2:20:1089:0:14:72:369:700:12:Times:12:Times:Consumption Risk and Human Capital Accumulation in India
2:21:1145:14:14:72:251:686:12:Times:12:Times:Andrew D. Foster and Esther Gehrke
2:22:1179:14:14:72:234:672:12:Times:12:Times:NBER Working Paper No. 24041
2:23:1207:14:14:72:150:658:12:Times:12:Times:November 2017
2:24:1220:14:28:72:178:644:12:Times:12:Times:JEL No. I25,O13,O15
2:25:1239:28:28:273:338:616:12:Times:12:Times:ABSTRACT
2:26:1247:28:14:72:546:588:12:Times:12:Times:We  study  the  consequences  of  dynamic  complementarities  in  the  production  of  child  human 
2:27:1347:14:14:72:545:574:12:Times:12:Times:capital for  the  relationship  between  risk  and  schooling  investment  in  a  low  income  setting.  In 
2:28:1454:14:14:72:546:560:12:Times:12:Times:contrast to previous literature, we explore the ex ante response of schooling to risk. We develop a 
2:29:1553:14:14:71:546:546:12:Times:12:Times:model that incorporates, dynamic complementarity in the education production function, ex-post 
2:30:1647:14:14:71:546:532:12:Times:12:Times:labor  market  responses  by mothers  to  income  shocks,  and  substitutability  between  maternal  and 
2:31:1751:14:14:72:546:518:12:Times:12:Times:child work in the household. We test the model using data from rural India, focusing particularly 
2:32:1848:14:14:72:545:504:12:Times:12:Times:on the schooling of girls. We find that risk substantially reduces the probability that girls attend 
2:33:1948:14:14:72:545:490:12:Times:12:Times:school.  We  then  simulate  the  effects of  an  implicit  social  insurance  program,  modeled  after  the 
2:34:2056:14:14:72:543:476:12:Times:12:Times:National  Rural  Employment  Guarantee Scheme  (NREGS).  Our  results  suggest  that  the  risk-
2:35:2151:14:14:72:546:462:12:Times:12:Times:reducing  effect  of  the  NREGS  may  offset  adverse effects  on  child  education  that  were  evident 
2:36:2256:14:42:72:306:448:12:Times:12:Times:during the NREGS phase-in due to rising wages.
2:37:2302:42:14:72:158:406:12:Times:12:Times:Andrew D. Foster
2:38:2318:14:14:72:314:392:12:Times:12:Times:Department of Economics and Community Health
2:39:2362:14:14:72:158:378:12:Times:12:Times:Brown University
2:40:2378:14:14:72:168:364:12:Times:12:Times:64 Waterman Street
2:41:2396:14:14:72:177:350:12:Times:12:Times:Providence, RI 02912
2:42:2416:14:14:72:124:336:12:Times:12:Times:and NBER
2:43:2424:14:28:72:167:322:12:Times:12:Times:afoster@brown.edu
2:44:2441:28:14:72:141:294:12:Times:12:Times:Esther Gehrke
2:45:2454:14:14:72:192:280:12:g_d0_f1:12:g_d0_f1:University of Goettingen
2:46:2478:14:14:72:306:266:12:Times:12:Times:Department of Agriculture and Rural Economics
2:47:2523:14:14:72:215:252:12:Times:12:Times:Platz der Goettingen Sieben 5
2:48:2552:14:14:72:209:238:12:Times:12:Times:37073 Goettingen, Germany
2:49:2577:14:0:72:232:224:12:Times:12:Times:esther.gehrke@uni-goettingen.de
3:50:2608:0:28:72:185:709:14:g_d0_f2:14:g_d0_f2:1    Introduction
3:51:2625:28:16:72:540:681:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:Accumulating human capital is an important path to participating in a country’s growth process,
3:52:2720:16:17:72:540:665:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:escaping poverty,  and achieving higher income and prosperity.  However,  we still lack conclusive
3:53:2818:17:16:72:540:648:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:knowledge of how human capital is formed, and what determines the investment in human capital,
3:54:2912:16:16:72:310:632:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:and schooling in particular, within the household.
3:55:2962:16:17:88:539:616:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:It is by now well established that economic shocks affect children’s time in school in the absence
3:56:3060:17:16:72:540:599:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:of well-functioning markets.  Jacoby and Skoufias (1997) show in the context of India that children
3:57:3159:16:17:72:540:583:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:have  to  drop-out  of  school  and  work  in  case  of  severe  economic  shocks.   Similar  evidence  was
3:58:3266:17:16:72:540:566:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:provided  by  Beegle  et  al.  (2006)  in  the  context  of  Tanzania  and  by  Duryea  et  al.  (2007)  in  the
3:59:3378:16:17:72:540:550:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:context of Brazil.  A related strand of literature has established that children’s school time falls in
3:60:3481:17:16:72:540:533:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:the aftermath of economic shocks because households lack the financial resources to send children
3:61:3578:16:16:72:500:517:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:to school (see e.g. Jensen, 2000; Skoufias and Parker, 2006; Gubert and Robilliard, 2008).
3:62:3668:16:17:88:539:501:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:Much less attention has been devoted to the possibleex anteeffects of risk on schooling decisions
3:63:3765:17:16:71:539:484:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:and human capital accumulation.  Recent works make a strong argument for dynamic complemen-
3:64:3856:16:17:71:539:468:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:tarities in the production of human capital (c.f. Cunha and Heckman, 2007; Cunha et al., 2010;
3:65:3950:17:16:71:539:451:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:Attanasio et al., 2015).  Dynamic complementarity arises when school investments for a given child
3:66:4048:16:17:71:539:435:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:in different periods are complements in the production of human capital.  In such a scenario, the
3:67:4145:17:16:71:539:418:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:returns  to  each  year  of  school  depend  on  past  as  well  as  on  future  investments  in  school.   This
3:68:4257:16:17:71:539:402:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:relationship also implies that early stage investments in education have to be followed up by in-
3:69:4354:17:16:71:539:385:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:vestments at later stages in order to make that early stage investment productive.  However, the
3:70:4450:16:16:71:539:369:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:ability to follow-up on this investment may depend on the realization of shocks in the future.  If
3:71:4548:16:17:71:539:353:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:future school time is not known, then households may be reluctant to invest in schooling in early
3:72:4645:17:16:71:539:336:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:periods, as they are uncertain about the ability to capture the returns to this investment at later
3:73:4744:16:17:71:539:320:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:stages.  Under such circumstances, the cost of risk in terms of human capital accumulation would
3:74:4840:17:16:71:391:303:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:be considerably higher than the sum of the direct effects of shocks.
3:75:4908:16:17:88:539:287:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:A reduction in school time as response to shocks might not only arise from child labor or lack
3:76:5002:17:16:71:539:270:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:in  financial  resources.   Evidence  suggests  that  households  tend  to  increase  their  labor  supply  in
3:77:5112:16:16:71:539:254:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:response to adverse economic shocks (Kochar, 1999; Rose, 2001; Jayachandran, 2006).  If women
3:78:5205:16:17:71:539:238:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:work more in the aftermath of economic shocks, they might have less time available for household
3:79:5301:17:16:71:539:221:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:activities.   In  the  presence  of  intra-household  substitution,  for  instance  in  the  performance  of
3:80:5409:16:17:71:539:205:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:household chores, increased female labor supply could have negative effects on the time allocation
3:81:5507:17:16:71:539:188:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:of their children.  As women have less time available for home production and childcare, and such
3:82:5604:16:17:71:539:172:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:activities can only be foregone at high cost, they might be forced to take older children out of school
3:83:5707:17:16:71:539:155:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:or to cut down on the time they study at home in order for them to fill in for these tasks (Skoufias,
3:84:5808:16:16:71:539:139:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:1993; Ilahi, 2000).1Given that women in India are responsible for most home-production activities,
3:85:5906:16:16:71:539:123:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:a labor supply response of mothers might in fact have larger intra-household repercussions than a
3:86:6003:16:15:84:539:107:6:g_d0_f6:9:g_d0_f7:1Shah and Steinberg (2015) for example show that girls in rural India are more likely to drop out of school if their
3:87:6119:15:0:72:239:92:9:g_d0_f7:9:g_d0_f7:mothers’ experience an increase in wages.
4:88:6161:0:17:72:540:709:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:labor supply response of fathers.2This also implies that risk might affect the time children spend
4:89:6259:17:16:72:525:692:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:in school even in the absence of a child labor market for girls and in contexts of no school fees.
4:90:6357:16:17:88:539:676:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:We study this question in the context in rural India and for girls.  We focus on girls for three
4:91:6453:17:16:72:540:659:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:reasons.  First, previous literature has emphasized the effect of shocks on boys’ labor supply and
4:92:6551:16:17:72:540:643:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:time in school, as they are more likely to be working outside the home than girls.  But while girls are
4:93:6654:17:16:72:540:626:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:not often found to be working outside the home, they are more likely to be performing household
4:94:6749:16:17:72:539:610:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:chores.  Second, our data suggest that mothers respond to shocks by supplying more laborex post,
4:95:6845:17:16:72:540:593:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:but not fathers.  And given the gendered nature of work in India in general, and of housework in
4:96:6941:16:16:72:540:577:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:particular, girl and mother time are likely to be more closely linked than are boy and mother time,
4:97:7040:16:17:72:540:561:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:giving us an opportunity to look more closely at underlying mechanisms for any observed responses.
4:98:7138:17:16:72:540:544:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:Finally, and perhaps most importantly, we have no clear way, within the village, of distinguishing
4:99:7236:16:17:72:540:528:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:contemporary effects of risk and future effects of risk.  Because of norms of patrilocal exogamy we
4:100:7335:17:16:72:540:511:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:cannot, for men, separately identify the current consumption-smoothing effects of risk on schooling
4:101:7434:16:17:72:540:495:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:from longer-term effects of risk on the returns to human capital.  For women, however, who will
4:102:7529:17:16:72:540:478:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:typically leave the village for marriage, we can control for the latter effects by conditioning on risk
4:103:7632:16:16:72:540:462:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:at the level of aggregation of the marriage market (as in Foster and Rosenzweig, 1999) and thus
4:104:7727:16:17:72:239:446:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:isolate the contemporaneous effect.
4:105:7762:17:16:88:539:429:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:This  paper  develops  a  model  of  household  time  allocation  and  human  capital  accumulation
4:106:7861:16:17:72:540:413:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:that  highlights  the  effect  of  uncertainty  regarding  future  consumption  on  a  child’s  school  time.
4:107:7970:17:16:72:540:396:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:Following  Jacoby  and  Skoufias  (1997),  we  model  schooling  decisions  in  a  unitary  household.   In
4:108:8077:16:17:72:540:380:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:order  to  incorporate  time  allocation  to  home  production,  we  assume  that  the  household  derives
4:109:8183:17:16:72:540:363:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:utility  from  two  consumption  goods,  one  of  which  is  produced  at  home  and  the  other  on  the
4:110:8288:16:16:72:540:347:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:market.   We  also  assume  that  women  increase  their  labor  supply  in  order  to  cope  with  income
4:111:8394:16:17:72:540:331:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:shocksex  post.  As both the mother and the daughter can perform household chores, shocks not
4:112:8487:17:16:72:540:314:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:only affect labor supply decisions of adult household members, but also reduce the time girls spend
4:113:8586:16:17:72:117:298:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:in school.
4:114:8596:17:16:88:539:281:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:By incorporating two periods into the model we are able to show that the presence of risk re-
4:115:8689:16:17:72:540:265:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:duces the time girls spend in school to the extent that marginal utility of second period schooling is
4:116:8791:17:16:72:540:248:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:increasing in first-period schooling.  Whether this is true depends both on the degree of complemen-
4:117:8891:16:17:72:540:232:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:tarity in the education production function and the extent of concavity in the preferences for child
4:118:8991:17:16:72:540:215:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:human  capital.   In  particular,  if  household  utility  is  linear  in  child-human  capital  then  negative
4:119:9102:16:16:72:540:199:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:effects are observed except when child school times in different periods are perfect substitutes in
4:120:9201:16:17:72:540:183:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:the production of human capital.  In contrast, if household utility is log-linear in human capital,
4:121:9300:17:16:72:540:166:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:the predicted effect of risk on school time is negative at high levels of dynamic complementarity
4:122:9397:16:16:72:386:150:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:but turns positive for elasticities of substitution greater than one.
4:123:9466:16:15:84:539:134:6:g_d0_f6:9:g_d0_f7:2The India Time Use survey from 1998/99 revealed that women spend more time than men on activities other
4:124:9570:15:11:72:540:119:9:g_d0_f7:9:g_d0_f7:than leisure.  While women spend 53.4 hours per week on salaried activities and household work combined, men only
4:125:9683:11:11:72:540:108:9:g_d0_f7:9:g_d0_f7:spend 45.6 hours per week on these activities (Government of India, 2000).  Women spend on average 18.7 hours
4:126:9792:11:11:72:540:97:9:g_d0_f7:9:g_d0_f7:per week on wage work and 34.6 hours per week on household-related activities.  These activities are classified as
4:127:9906:11:11:72:540:86:9:g_d0_f7:9:g_d0_f7:extended SNA activities in the Time Use Survey and include household maintenance, as well as care for children,
4:128:10017:11:0:72:137:75:9:g_d0_f7:9:g_d0_f7:sick and elderly.
5:129:10035:0:17:88:539:709:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:We therefore explore the case for dynamic complementarity in study hours among children in
5:130:10125:17:16:72:540:692:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:rural India directly by estimating a production function of cognitive skills.  In the absence of skills
5:131:10228:16:17:72:540:676:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:data  in  the  Rural  Economic  and  Demographic  Survey  (REDS),  we  use  the  Young  Lives  Survey
5:132:10329:17:16:72:540:659:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:(YLS)  data  from  Andhra  Pradesh,  a  state  in  the  south  of  India.   While  Attanasio  et  al.  (2015)
5:133:10438:16:17:72:540:643:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:have used these data for a similar study, that paper considers educational expenditures rather than
5:134:10537:17:16:72:540:626:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:study  time.   Using  a  non-linear  instrumental  variables  procedure,  we  cannot  reject  an  elasticity
5:135:10645:16:17:72:540:610:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:of substitution of one between lagged cognitive skills and current study hours in the production
5:136:10741:17:16:72:539:593:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f4:of current cognitive skills.  This elasticity is sufficient, given our model, to produce a negativeex
5:137:10842:16:16:72:539:577:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f3:anteeffect of risk on schooling if parental utility is linear in human capital but not, for example, if
5:138:10945:16:17:72:290:561:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:parental utility is log-linear in human capital.
5:139:10993:17:16:88:539:544:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:We then test the predictions of our model with the REDS data from 2006.  Our unit of observa-
5:140:11086:16:17:72:540:528:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:tion is the mother-daughter pair for girls aged 6 to 16 who live with their mothers.  We observe time
5:141:11187:17:16:72:540:511:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:allocation decision over three different agricultural seasons,  which have a length of four months.
5:142:11286:16:17:72:301:495:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:This gives a final sample of 12,918 observations.
5:143:11335:17:16:88:539:478:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:We predict consumption risk at the village level as a function of the historical rainfall distribution
5:144:11437:16:16:72:540:462:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:and a village’s share of land that is under irrigation.  Irrigation can mitigate some of the effects of
5:145:11540:16:17:72:540:446:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:adverse rainfall shocks on consumption, such that an increase in the availability of irrigation systems
5:146:11643:17:16:72:540:429:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:in a village contributes to a reduction in the variability of consumption.  Specifically, we estimate
5:147:11744:16:17:72:540:413:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:the relationship between consumption, rainfall, village-level agricultural area under irrigation, and
5:148:11845:17:16:72:540:396:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:the interaction of these two variables.  Using these estimates,  we predict consumption outcomes
5:149:11941:16:17:72:540:380:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:at  the  village  level  for  each  observed  rainfall  outcome,  given  the  current  share  of  area  that  is
5:150:12053:17:16:72:540:363:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:irrigated.   We  then  use  the  historical  rainfall  distribution  to  obtain  the  probability  distribution
5:151:12164:16:16:72:540:347:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:of rainfall outcomes, and then calculate the probability distribution of predicted consumption for
5:152:12262:16:17:72:540:331:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:each village.  Consumption risk, finally, is defined as the variability in the distribution of predicted
5:153:12366:17:16:72:540:314:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:consumption.  This concept of risk explores the fact that labor markets in rural India are largely
5:154:12464:16:17:72:540:298:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:dominated by agriculture at the time of the survey.  Hence, rainfall shocks not only affect farmers’
5:155:12564:17:16:72:540:281:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:consumption through on-farm production, they also affect consumption levels of landless households
5:156:12662:16:17:72:332:265:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:who mostly engage in casual agricultural employment.
5:157:12714:17:16:88:539:248:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:We find that consumption risk substantially reduces the probability that girls attend school.
5:158:12807:16:17:72:540:232:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:This effect is robust to controlling for average risk in sample villages belonging to the same marriage
5:159:12910:17:16:72:540:215:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:market.  Consistent with our theoretical model, we also find that risk increases the probability that
5:160:13011:16:16:72:540:199:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:girls perform household chores,  and stronger effects for older girls.  Our findings are also robust
5:161:13111:16:17:72:540:183:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:to controlling flexibly for household income and wealth, as well as for the share of area irrigated
5:162:13210:17:16:72:540:166:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:on a household’s own farm for the sub-sample of farm-households.  We also test if our results are
5:163:13307:16:17:72:540:150:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:driven by a savings motive at the household level, acknowledging that one alternative explanation
5:164:13404:17:16:72:540:133:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:could be that households in more risky environments may wish to save more.  This would lead both
5:165:13500:16:17:72:540:117:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:men and women to work more,  such that the observed effect on girls would really be driven by
5:166:13593:17:16:72:540:100:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:an increase in maternal labor supply, not dynamic complementarity in the education production
5:167:13686:16:0:72:540:84:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:function.  We find no evidence that household savings is higher in risk-prone areas.  Finally, we test
6:168:13789:0:17:72:540:709:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:if returns to education or the supply of education vary with risk, again finding no evidence that
6:169:13886:17:16:72:251:692:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:these factors may explain our results.
6:170:13924:16:17:88:539:676:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:In order to assess the scope for public policy, we simulate the effects of an income-smoothing
6:171:14018:17:16:72:540:659:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:policy, modeled after the National Rural Employment Guarantee Scheme (NREGS) in India, on
6:172:14107:16:17:72:539:643:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:human capital accumulation.  We argue that the policy makes anex  postlabor supply response
6:173:14198:17:16:71:539:626:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:to  shocks  more  effective  by  mitigating  the  negative  effect  of  shocks  on  wages.   We  estimate  the
6:174:14308:16:17:71:539:610:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:extent to which NREGS reduces variability in consumption and use this reduction in consumption
6:175:14402:17:16:71:539:593:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:risk to simulate the effect of the program on girls’ probability to attend school.  We find that a
6:176:14500:16:16:71:539:577:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:similar program, that held the level of wages fixed, would increase school attendance of girls by 2.9
6:177:14601:16:17:71:158:561:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:percentage points.
6:178:14619:17:16:88:539:544:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:The wage effects if such programs should not be ignored, however.  Shah and Steinberg (2015)
6:179:14711:16:17:71:539:528:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:and Li and Sekhri (2013) find negative effects of the NREGS on the time girls spend in school using
6:180:14810:17:16:71:539:511:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:the rolled phase-in of NREGS as a source of variation.  It is argued that the NREGS increased the
6:181:14907:16:17:71:539:495:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:opportunity costs of time of adolescents and therefore led them to drop out of school at younger
6:182:15003:17:16:71:540:478:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:ages.3Arguably a risk mitigation effect could not be fully internalized by households at the early
6:183:15101:16:16:71:539:462:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:stages of the program when implementation was spotty and long-term viability was unclear.  It is
6:184:15197:16:17:71:539:446:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:unclear how the direct effect of rising wages and the indirect effect of less variable incomes balance
6:185:15299:17:16:71:539:429:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:out in the longer term.  However, our results suggest the negative wage consequences for schooling
6:186:15397:16:17:71:539:413:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:of the NREGS might be mitigated to the extent that the program is recognized as a reliable source
6:187:15494:17:16:71:282:396:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:of support during periods of adverse shocks.
6:188:15538:16:17:88:539:380:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:This paper links two significant strands in the development literature.  The first, focuses on the
6:189:15636:17:16:71:539:363:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:role of risk as a constraint to productive investment in low-income countries (see e.g. Rosenzweig
6:190:15734:16:16:71:539:347:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:and Binswanger, 1993; Fafchamps and Pender, 1997; Dercon and Christiaensen, 2011; Bianchi and
6:191:15827:16:17:71:539:331:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:Bobba, 2013; Karlan et al., 2014).  Consistent with much of this literature we find that risk reduces
6:192:15928:17:16:71:539:314:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:investment.   The  second  strand  considers  the  determinants  of  human  capital  investment  in  low-
6:193:16033:16:17:71:539:298:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:income countries (see e.g. Foster and Rosenzweig, 1996; Glewwe and Jacoby, 2004; Jensen, 2010,
6:194:16127:17:16:71:539:281:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:2012; Oster and Steinberg, 2013).  As noted previously,  there is other work that focuses on how
6:195:16223:16:17:71:539:265:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:income realizations affect schooling investment in a setting where school investments at different
6:196:16321:17:16:71:539:248:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:points in time are complementary.  However,  we are not aware of other work that considers the
6:197:16415:16:17:71:251:232:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:question from anex anteperspective.
6:198:16450:17:16:88:539:215:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:The  remainder  of  the  paper  proceeds  as  follows.   Section  2  discusses  the  theoretical  model.
6:199:16554:16:16:71:539:199:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:Section 3 presents evidence for dynamic complementarity in the production of human capital in
6:200:16647:16:17:71:539:183:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:India.  The data are introduced in Section 4, and the estimation strategy in Section 5.  Results are
6:201:16747:17:16:71:539:166:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:discussed in Section 6.  Section 7 simulates the effect of the NREGS on the outcome of interest,
6:202:16843:16:14:71:188:150:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:and Section 8 concludes.
6:203:16867:14:15:84:539:136:6:g_d0_f6:9:g_d0_f7:3This effect seems to work through intra-household substitution in household chores for girls,  and through the
6:204:16978:15:0:72:162:121:9:g_d0_f7:9:g_d0_f7:labor market for boys.
7:205:17001:0:22:72:540:709:14:g_d0_f2:14:g_d0_f2:2    A model of household time allocation, risk and human capital
7:206:17066:22:27:96:173:687:14:g_d0_f2:14:g_d0_f2:investment
7:207:17076:27:17:72:540:660:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:Our model helps to illustrate the basic mechanisms of anex anteschooling response to risk that is
7:208:17173:17:16:72:540:643:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:particularly relevant to the circumstances of girls in rural India.  It captures, in particular, three im-
7:209:17279:16:17:72:540:627:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:portant features that may lead to a relationship between consumption risk and child schooling.  The
7:210:17378:17:16:72:540:610:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:first is dynamic complementarity in the education production function as in Jacoby and Skoufias
7:211:17473:16:17:72:540:594:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:(1997); Cunha and Heckman (2007); Cunha et al. (2010).  As a consequence of this complemen-
7:212:17564:17:16:72:540:577:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:tarity households may be reluctant to make initial investments in schooling if they are uncertain
7:213:17661:16:16:72:540:561:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:about the ability to capture the returns to this investment when schooling may be interrupted as
7:214:17757:16:17:72:539:545:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:a consequence of a negative shock.  The second feature is anex postlabor supply response on the
7:215:17852:17:16:72:540:528:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:part of the mother.  The idea is that a mother will enter the labor market in the face of an adverse
7:216:17952:16:17:72:540:512:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:shock (Kochar, 1999; Rose, 2001; Jayachandran, 2006).  The third feature is a substitution between
7:217:18050:17:16:72:540:495:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:the mother’s and her daughter’s time in home production.  The idea here is that the opportunity
7:218:18145:16:17:72:540:479:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:cost of time for a daughter must rise during bad periods even though she is unlikely to be active
7:219:18242:17:16:72:304:462:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:in the labor market (Skoufias, 1993; Ilahi, 2000).
7:220:18292:16:17:88:539:446:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:We consider a two period model where utilityuin each of the two periods is defined over market
7:221:18386:17:16:72:539:429:11:g_d0_f8:11:g_d0_f3:cmand homechgoods, with an additional second period term that is defined as a functionvof the
7:222:18479:16:30:72:401:413:11:g_d0_f3:8:g_d0_f5:eventual stock of human capitalH2at the end of the second period:4
7:223:18545:30:29:99:540:383:11:g_d0_f8:11:g_d0_f3:L=u(cm1,ch1) +δE[u(cm2,ch2) +v(H2)].(1)
7:224:18584:29:17:72:540:354:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:The human capital stock in period 2 increases in school time in periods 1 and 2 according to a
7:225:18678:17:30:72:366:337:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:constant elasticity of substitution (CES) production function:
7:226:18740:30:29:99:540:307:11:g_d0_f8:11:g_d0_f3:H2=A[νsρ1+ (1−ν)sρ2]1/ρ,(2)
7:227:18767:29:17:72:540:278:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:forν∈[0,1] andρ∈[−∞,1].Ais the total factor productivity, andνthe share parameter, also
7:228:18854:17:16:72:539:261:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:described asskill multiplierin Cunha and Heckman (2007). The degree of dynamic complementarity
7:229:18948:16:17:72:540:245:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:is entirely governed byρ, whereσ= 1/(1−ρ) is the elasticity of substitution between school time
7:230:19043:17:16:72:539:228:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:in periods 1 and 2.  The single-period utility functionuis assumed to be log Cobb-Douglas with
7:231:19137:16:30:72:162:212:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:share parameterα:
7:232:19154:30:30:99:540:182:11:g_d0_f8:11:g_d0_f3:u(cm,ch) =αlncm+ (1−α) lnch.(3)
7:233:19185:30:16:72:540:152:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:Market goods are purchased using cash income that is earned by the mother with wagewtand a
7:234:19275:16:16:72:539:136:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:time allocation ofTa−hat, whereTais her time endowment andhatis the time spent by the mother
7:235:19367:16:15:84:539:120:6:g_d0_f6:9:g_d0_f7:4Because we focus on girls who are likely to leave the village for marriage, we assume that the returns to human
7:236:19479:15:0:72:242:105:9:g_d0_f7:9:g_d0_f7:capital are unaffected by village-level risk.
8:237:19525:0:30:72:169:709:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:in home production,
8:238:19544:30:30:99:540:679:11:g_d0_f8:11:g_d0_f3:cmt=It+θt+wt(Ta−hat).(4)
8:239:19568:30:16:71:539:649:11:g_d0_f8:11:g_d0_f3:Itis the non-stochastic part of income (the father’s income, where the father is assumed to be full
8:240:19667:16:17:71:540:633:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:time in the market or any non-labor income), andθtis a shock realized at timet.  Consumption of
8:241:19762:17:30:71:539:616:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:the home-produced good is linear in the allocation of time by the motherhatand the daughterhct,
8:242:19857:30:29:99:540:586:11:g_d0_f8:11:g_d0_f3:cht=hat+hct,(5)
8:243:19872:29:17:71:539:557:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:and school time is the difference between the daughter’s time endowmentTcand the amount of
8:244:19962:17:29:71:222:540:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:time spent in home production:
8:245:19992:29:30:99:540:511:11:g_d0_f8:11:g_d0_f3:st=Tc−hct.(6)
8:246:20005:30:17:71:539:481:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:Finally, the shockθtis zero in the first period, and takes on two values [−r,r] with equal probability
8:247:20107:17:16:71:539:464:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:in the second period.  Note that we do not allow the shock to affect the wage; adding such an effect
8:248:20207:16:16:71:539:448:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:substantially increases the analytic complexity as the price and income effects of wages move in
8:249:20303:16:17:71:539:432:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:opposite directions.  Our empirical results suggest that the income effects dominate, i.e.  mothers
8:250:20402:17:16:71:296:415:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:tend to work more when facing adverse shocks.
8:251:20447:16:17:88:539:399:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:We first solve for second period maternal home time by equating the marginal return in the
8:252:20537:17:29:71:252:382:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:market to the marginal time at home:

8:254:20611:30:17:71:539:323:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:The result is decreasing in the daughter’s home time, increasing in the shock, and decreasing in the
8:255:20711:17:16:71:539:306:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:wage.  With a more favorable shock or a lower wage, mothers spend less time in the labor market
8:256:20806:16:30:71:418:290:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:and more time at home.  Similarly first period maternal time at home is:
8:257:20878:30:30:99:540:260:11:g_d0_f8:11:g_d0_f3:ha1= (1−α)Ta−αhc1+ (1−α)I1/w1.(8)
8:258:20911:30:16:88:539:230:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:To develop an intuition for how risk affects first-period school time in the presence of dynamic
8:259:21007:16:16:71:539:214:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:complementarity, we first consider the extreme of a production function with perfect complements
8:260:21103:16:17:71:539:198:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:(ρ→−∞) and wherev(H2) =H2/ζis linear in the stock of human capital.  We will subsequently
8:261:21192:17:16:71:535:181:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:numerically illustrate the effects for intermediate values ofρand alternative specifications ofv().
8:262:21291:16:17:88:539:165:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:When first and second period school times are perfect complements, second period home time of
8:263:21384:17:16:71:539:148:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:the daughter will depend on whether or not school investment in the second period is constrained by
8:264:21483:16:17:71:539:132:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:first period school time. By “constrained” we mean that, due to the complementarity in production,
8:265:21581:17:0:71:539:115:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:if  first  period  schooling  were  exogenously  higher,  thenceteris  paribusthe  household  would  also
9:266:21687:0:30:72:497:709:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:choose higher second period schooling.  In this sense the household will be constrained if:
9:267:21778:30:30:99:540:679:11:g_d0_f8:11:g_d0_f3:θ2> w2(ζ−Ta−hc1)−I2,(9)
9:268:21801:30:30:71:134:649:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:in which case
9:269:21814:30:29:99:539:619:11:g_d0_f8:11:g_d0_f3:hc2=hc1.(10)
9:270:21826:29:30:72:123:590:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:Otherwise,
9:271:21836:30:30:99:539:560:11:g_d0_f8:11:g_d0_f3:hc2=ζ−Ta−(I2+θ2)/w2.(11)
9:272:21860:30:16:72:540:530:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:Note that in the former, constrained case, child home time does not depend on the shock but in
9:273:21954:16:17:72:540:514:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:the second unconstrained case, child home time is decreasing in the shock.  Under either condition
9:274:22052:17:16:72:297:497:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:maternal home time is decreasing in the shock.
9:275:22098:16:17:88:540:481:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:By substitutingha2,ha1andhc1intoLand taking expectations over the assumed distribution of
9:276:22187:17:16:72:540:464:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:shocks, we can then write the maximand as a function of child home time in the first period only.
9:277:22284:16:16:72:540:448:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:This expression is straightforward in the case that the variance of the shock is small enough that

9:292:22902:0:32:289:539:333:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:+O(r3).(12)
9:293:22913:32:17:72:438:301:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:where that we assume thatI1=I2andw1=w2to keep the solution simple.
9:294:22979:17:16:88:540:284:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:Taking values for the parameters:Ta=Tc= 4,α=δ= 1/2,ζ= 4,I= 6, andw= 1, this
9:295:23054:16:30:71:166:268:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:expression becomes:
9:296:23073:30:29:99:539:238:11:g_d0_f8:11:g_d0_f3:hc1= 2 +r2/36 +O(r3).(13)
9:297:23098:29:17:72:540:209:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:The condition for second period schooling to be constrained by the first period schooling isr≤12;
9:298:23195:17:16:72:540:192:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:however, this implies home-time of 6, which exceeds the time allocation for the child.  Thus for an
9:299:23294:16:17:72:540:176:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:interior solution and these parameter values, we always have the constrained case and second period
9:300:23393:17:16:72:539:159:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:schooling is equal to first period schooling.  The exact numerical value forhc1is 2 (corresponding to
9:301:23494:16:17:72:437:143:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:schooling of 2) when there is no variance, and 3 (schooling of 1) whenr= 6.
9:302:23569:17:16:88:539:126:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:This result shows clearly that – in the constrained case – first period home time is increasing in
9:303:23667:16:17:72:540:110:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:the variance of the shock and thus conversely first period schooling is decreasing in the variance of
9:304:23768:17:16:72:540:93:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:the shock.  This result also implies that second period school time and thus the eventual stock of
9:305:23866:16:0:72:540:77:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:schooling is decreasing in the variance of the shock.  The intuition is simple.  If risk increases, then
10:306:23971:0:17:72:540:709:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:the marginal cost of second-period school time – in terms of foregone consumption of the home
10:307:24064:17:16:72:540:692:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:produced good – increases more in the event of a bad shock than it decreases in the event of a
10:308:24158:16:17:72:540:676:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:good shock.  Because, in the constrained case, second-period schooling is set equal to first period
10:309:24257:17:16:72:349:659:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:schooling, an increase in risk lowers first period schooling.
10:310:24318:16:17:88:539:643:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:Note that this is the opposite of a precautionary savings motive.  In a typical precautionary
10:311:24411:17:16:72:540:626:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:savings model, period one investment is a substitute for income in the second period, and thus one
10:312:24509:16:17:72:540:610:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:avoids the downside risk by reducing consumption in the first period.  Here in effect first period
10:313:24607:17:16:72:540:593:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:investment is a complement with second period investment, so the opposite occurs when faced with
10:314:24703:16:16:72:129:577:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:greater risk.
10:315:24716:16:17:88:539:561:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:If  the  variance  is  so  high  that  condition  (9)  is  not  met  in  the  bad  state  of  the  world,  then
10:316:24827:17:16:72:540:544:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:variance  can  have  the  opposite  effect  on  first  period  time  allocation.   In  particular,  in  the  good
10:317:24940:16:17:72:540:528:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:state of the world one obtains (10) and in the bad state of the world one obtains (11).  Thus the
10:318:25037:17:16:72:540:511:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:second  period  downside  risk  is  mitigated  because  it  is  no  longer  tied  to  the  first-period  level  of
10:319:25151:16:17:72:540:495:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:schooling.  On the other hand, the upside risk can fully be realized by setting first period equal to
10:320:25252:17:16:72:540:478:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:second period schooling.  In particular, the series approximation to first-period home time in the
10:321:25350:16:31:72:176:462:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:unconstrained case is:
10:322:25372:31:0:99:151:431:11:g_d0_f8:11:g_d0_f3:hc1=ζ(1 +
10:323:25381:0:15:154:159:439:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:2
10:324:25382:15:0:154:159:424:11:g_d0_f8:11:g_d0_f8:δ
10:325:25383:0:0:161:202:431:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f11:)−Ta−
10:326:25388:0:15:207:212:439:11:g_d0_f8:11:g_d0_f8:I

10:328:25390:0:0:218:226:431:11:g_d0_f11:11:g_d0_f11:−
10:329:25391:0:15:246:258:439:11:g_d0_f8:11:g_d0_f8:δ r
10:330:25394:15:0:230:274:424:11:g_d0_f8:11:g_d0_f3:w(δ+ 2)
10:331:25401:0:0:278:294:431:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:+ 2
10:332:25404:0:17:317:336:439:11:g_d0_f8:8:g_d0_f5:δ2r2

10:334:25418:0:33:360:539:431:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:+O(r3).(14)
10:335:25429:33:17:72:540:398:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:Note that first period home time falls inrand increases in the variance ofr.  The negative term
10:336:25524:17:16:72:540:381:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:may  dominate  if  the  ratioδ/wis  sufficiently  small.   This  result  has  an  analogy  with  respect  to
10:337:25632:16:17:72:540:365:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:borrowing in the case of investment risk:  increasing risk can increase the willingness to take a loan
10:338:25734:17:16:72:540:348:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:at a given interest rate because it raises the return in the good state and does not decrease it in
10:339:25833:16:16:72:274:332:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:the bad state because one simply defaults.
10:340:25875:16:17:88:539:316:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:More  generally,  the  effect  of  variance  on  first-period  investment  depends  on  whether  human
10:341:25977:17:16:72:540:299:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:capital demand exhibits dynamic complementarity, that is whether increases in first period school-
10:342:26075:16:17:72:540:283:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:ing lead to an increase in the marginal utility of second period schooling.  This depends, in turn,
10:343:26174:17:16:72:540:266:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:both on the elasticity of substitution of the human capital production function and on the extent
10:344:26271:16:17:72:540:250:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:to which parents exhibit diminishing marginal utility with respect to human capital through the
10:345:26366:17:31:72:232:233:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:functionv(H2).  In particular, let:
10:346:26401:31:0:99:138:202:11:g_d0_f8:11:g_d0_f3:v(H2) =
10:347:26408:0:15:152:157:209:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:1
10:348:26409:15:0:142:167:194:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f8:1−φ
10:349:26412:0:32:169:539:202:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:(H2)1−φ,(15)
10:350:26424:32:34:72:532:170:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:withφ >= 0.  By substituting (2) into (15) and differentiating with respect tos1ands2we find:
10:351:26517:34:0:99:124:136:11:g_d0_f8:11:g_d0_f3:sign(
10:352:26522:0:15:125:163:144:11:g_d0_f8:11:g_d0_f3:d2v(H2)
10:353:26529:15:0:129:159:129:11:g_d0_f8:8:g_d0_f5:ds1ds2
10:354:26535:0:31:165:539:136:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:) =sign(φ+ρ−1).(16)
10:355:26554:31:16:72:539:105:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:Thus forφ= 0, i.e.  linear utility with respect to child human capital, there will always be dynamic
10:356:26654:16:17:72:540:89:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:complementarity in demand, except the extreme case where the human capital production function
10:357:26748:17:0:72:539:72:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:exhibits perfect substitutability (ρ= 1).  On the other hand forφ= 1,  logarithmic utility,  time
11:358:26846:0:17:72:540:709:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:in school at different points will be dynamic complements if and only if the production function
11:359:26942:17:16:72:540:692:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:exhibits complementarity (ρ <0).  Finally, ifφ= 2 then school investments at different points in
11:360:27038:16:17:72:284:676:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:time will be dynamic substitutes forρ >−1.
11:361:27080:17:16:88:539:659:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:Although we are unable to derive analytic solutions for the effects of risk on first-period schooling
11:362:27181:16:17:71:539:643:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:in  the  general  CES  case,  numerical  solutions  suggest  that  the  sign  of  the  effect  of  variance  on
11:363:27292:17:16:71:539:626:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:first-period human capital investment maps 1:1 with the presence of dynamic complementarity in
11:364:27386:16:17:71:293:610:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:demand for human capital as described above.
11:365:27430:17:16:88:539:593:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:Figure 1 plots the derivative of first period schooling with respect to the magnitude of the shock
11:366:27528:16:16:71:539:577:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:against the log of the elasticity of substitutionσ= 1/(1−ρ).5It shows outcomes for three functional
11:367:27627:16:17:71:539:561:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:forms, linear utility in human capital, and two alternative functions that allow for risk-aversion in
11:368:27728:17:16:71:539:544:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:human  capital:v(H2)  =ln(H2)  andv(H2)  =−1/H2.   Since  we  are  plotting  against  ln(σ),  the
11:369:27825:16:17:71:540:528:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:y-axis  captures  the  Cobb-Douglas  case.   Asln(σ)→1.5,  the  production  function  approaches  a
11:370:27924:17:16:71:539:511:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:simple additive function in school times, i.e.  the case of perfect substitutes.  And asln(σ)→−1.5,
11:371:28023:16:17:71:478:495:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:the human capital production function exhibits increasing dynamic complementarity.
11:372:28105:17:16:88:539:478:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:As  can  be  seen,  the  predicted effect  of  risk  is strictly  negative  as  long  as the  human  capital
11:373:28213:16:16:71:539:462:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:production function exhibits strong complementarity.  For more moderate values ofρ, the picture
11:374:28308:16:17:71:539:446:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:is somewhat more complex.  With linearvthe predicted effect of risk remains negative throughout,
11:375:28404:17:16:71:539:429:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:and approaches zero only as the human capital production function exhibits perfect substitutability
11:376:28503:16:17:71:539:413:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:(ρ= 1).  With concavev, this is no longer the case.  Second period risk will then affect the expected
11:377:28604:17:16:71:539:396:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:marginal utility of the human capital stock, and households will rather invest more in schooling in
11:378:28703:16:17:71:539:380:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:order to avoid very low stocks of human capital for their children.  Ifv(H2) =ln(H2), for example,
11:379:28801:17:16:71:540:363:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:first period schooling will increase in risk for allρ >0.  Ifv(H2) =−1/H2, the predicted effect of
11:380:28899:16:16:72:325:347:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:risk becomes positive already at negative values ofρ.
11:381:28952:16:17:88:539:331:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:Whether variance increases or decreases first-period investment thus depends on the presence
11:382:29044:17:16:71:539:314:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:of  dynamic  complementarity  in  the  demand  for  human  capital,  which  depends  in  turn,  at  least
11:383:29149:16:17:71:539:298:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:in part, on parental preferences for child human capital.  While we can (and do, below) directly
11:384:29245:17:16:71:539:281:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:estimate a production function, the shape of human capital preferences is unclear.  If we think about
11:385:29346:16:17:71:539:265:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:equation (1) as overlapping-generations problem, we would expect parents to exhibit diminishing
11:386:29441:17:16:71:539:248:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:marginal utility with respect to the future consumption of their child,  such as in Becker (1991).
11:387:29539:16:17:71:539:232:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:But we are not maximizing household utility over the daughter’s future consumption, but her final
11:388:29636:17:16:71:539:215:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:stock of human capital.  How the stock of human capital then translates into future earnings and
11:389:29732:16:16:71:539:199:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:consumption  is  essentially  an  empirical  question.   While  returns  to  schooling  seem  to  be  rather
11:390:29840:16:17:71:539:183:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:linear  in  reality  (Banerjee  and  Duflo,  2005),  increasing  evidence  suggests  that  households  have
11:391:29947:17:16:71:539:166:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:inconsistent expectations regarding the exact relationship between years of schooling and future
11:392:30043:16:17:71:539:150:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:income.  Many households seem to expect that marginal returns to schooling increase with years
11:393:30137:17:16:71:539:133:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:of schooling (Banerjee and Duflo, 2011; Barrera-Osorio et al., 2011; Akresh et al., 2012).  A linear
11:394:30237:16:16:71:539:117:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:utility in human capital might thus be a reasonable approximation of household decision making
11:395:30331:16:15:84:540:101:6:g_d0_f6:9:g_d0_f7:5Similarly to above we choose the parameter values ofTa=Tc= 4,α=δ= 1/2,I= 6,ζ= 4,  andw= 1.
11:396:30422:15:0:72:306:86:9:g_d0_f7:9:g_d0_f7:Furthermore we setr= 6 and the skill multiplierν= 1/2.
12:397:30477:0:17:72:540:709:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:if we combine the expectations of increasing returns to human capital with decreasing marginal
12:398:30571:17:38:72:266:692:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:utility of the child’s future consumption.
12:399:30613:38:27:72:532:654:14:g_d0_f2:14:g_d0_f2:3    Estimating the elasticity of substitution between school times
12:400:30680:27:17:72:540:627:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:One important component of the predicted effect of risk is the degree of complementarity between
12:401:30776:17:16:72:539:610:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:each period’s school times.  An exact estimate ofρwould allow us to make more sensible predictions
12:402:30874:16:17:71:256:594:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:about the effect risk on time in school.
12:403:30914:17:16:88:540:577:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:In order to estimateρ, we need measures of skills and investment.  Given that the data used in
12:404:31008:16:16:71:539:561:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:this paper does not contain information about skills, we have to draw on a different data set from
12:405:31106:16:17:71:539:545:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:India, the Young Lives Survey.  The Young Lives project focuses on child development and follows
12:406:31202:17:16:71:539:528:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:children from two different cohorts in four different countries since 2002.  For this study, we use the
12:407:31305:16:17:71:259:512:11:g_d0_f3:8:g_d0_f5:India data from rounds two and three.6
12:408:31343:17:16:88:539:495:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:We follow Cunha and Heckman (2007), in specifying a constant elasticity of substitution pro-
12:409:31435:16:17:71:539:479:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:duction function, in which the stock of human capital, more precisely of cognitive skills, increases
12:410:31535:17:16:71:539:462:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:in the initial stock of human capitalH1, current investmentss2, and the parental stock of human
12:411:31630:16:30:72:124:446:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:capitalHp:
12:412:31640:30:30:99:539:416:11:g_d0_f8:11:g_d0_f3:H2=A[ν1Hρ1+ν2sρ2+ν3Hρp]1/ρ.(17)
12:413:31671:30:0:72:305:386:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:A is the total factor productivity.νi∈[0,1] and
12:414:31718:0:12:309:321:395:11:g_d0_f15:11:g_d0_f15:∑
12:415:31719:12:13:321:540:383:8:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f3:1νi= 1 fori∈ {1,2,3}.σ= 1/(1−ρ) is the
12:416:31757:13:16:72:540:370:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:elasticity of substitution between inputs and skills in the production of outcomes,  and measures
12:417:31854:16:17:72:524:354:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:how easily previously accumulated stocks of skills can be compensated by current investments.
12:418:31947:17:16:88:539:337:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:Skills are likely measured with error.  Therefore, we follow Cunha et al. (2010) in their approach
12:419:32045:16:17:72:540:321:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:of estimating a skills production function by first estimating a set of latent factors (H1,H2and
12:420:32141:17:16:72:540:304:11:g_d0_f8:11:g_d0_f3:Hp) from multiple measurements of skills (Zj).  This allows to identify the structural parameters of
12:421:32241:16:17:72:540:288:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:eq.  (17) even if skills are measured with error.  To keep the problem simple, we specify linear factor
12:422:32344:17:16:72:539:271:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:models,  and  as  in  Cunha  et  al.  (2010)  we  assume  that  the  measurementsZjproxy  the  natural
12:423:32446:16:30:72:277:255:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:logarithms of the factors, such that forH1:
12:424:32489:30:30:99:539:225:11:g_d0_f8:11:g_d0_f3:Z1j=μ1j+α1jlnH1+-1j.(18)
12:425:32513:30:16:88:539:195:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:We also allow for endogeneity of investment and skills in (17) by instrumenting both variables
12:426:32607:16:16:72:540:179:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:with rainfall, land ownership and the interaction of the two. We find that rainfall raises wages, which
12:427:32710:16:17:72:540:163:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:draws children out of school, but this pressure falls with wealth, here captured by land ownership.
12:428:32809:17:16:72:540:146:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:The identifying assumption is that conditional on parental skills,  neither land ownership nor its
12:429:32907:16:16:72:540:130:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:interaction with rainfall has a direct effect on skills or investment.  Assuming that the identifying
12:430:33008:16:0:84:405:114:6:g_d0_f6:9:g_d0_f7:6More details on the data and estimation strategy are available in Appendix A.
13:431:33088:0:30:72:442:709:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:assumption is valid, eq.  (17) can be identified in a control function approach,
13:432:33168:30:30:99:539:679:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:ln(H2) =A+ 1/ρln(ν1Hρ1+ν2sρ2+ν3Hρp) +κ1γh+κ2γs,(19)
13:433:33219:30:16:72:539:649:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:whereγhis the residual of the investment equation andγsis the residual of the skills equation.
13:434:33313:16:17:72:540:633:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:Results can be found in Table 1.  Columns (1) - (3) report results for the younger cohort, aged 5
13:435:33410:17:16:72:540:616:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:in round 2 (2007) and aged 8 in round 3 (2009-10).  Columns (4)-(6) report results for the older
13:436:33506:16:17:72:539:600:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:cohort, aged 12 in 2007 and 15 in 2009-10.7As can be seen the results are fairly similar for both
13:437:33603:17:16:72:540:583:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:cohorts and irrespectively of whether we allow investments, or investments and initial skills to be
13:438:33702:16:17:72:539:567:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:endogenous.  The self-productivity parameterν1is 0.26 in the younger cohort and 0.64 is the older
13:439:33799:17:16:72:540:550:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:cohort.   The  elasticity  of  substitutionσis  close  to  one  for  both  cohorts:  it  varies  between  0.91
13:440:33909:16:16:72:540:534:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:and 1.06 for the younger cohort and between 0.92 and 1.16 for the older cohort.  The results thus
13:441:34006:16:17:72:540:518:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:suggest that the human capital production function can best be approximated by a Cobb-Douglas
13:442:34099:17:16:72:478:501:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:production function, while we can strongly reject perfect substitutability in all cases.
13:443:34187:16:17:88:539:485:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:These results are well in line with Cunha et al. (2010) who use data from the US, as well as
13:444:34279:17:16:72:540:468:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:with Attanasio et al. (2015), who also use the Young Lives Survey data from India.  While we focus
13:445:34377:16:17:72:540:452:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:on children’s study time per day as key investment variable, Attanasio et al. (2015) use spending
13:446:34474:17:16:72:540:435:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:on school as well as the number of meals per day to proxy investment.  Not surprisingly, the results
13:447:34574:16:38:72:148:419:11:g_d0_f3:8:g_d0_f5:closely overlap.8
13:448:34591:38:27:72:220:381:14:g_d0_f2:14:g_d0_f2:4    Context and data
13:449:34612:27:17:72:540:354:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:We test if risk affects school time with the 2006 round of the Rural Economic and Demographic
13:450:34705:17:16:72:540:337:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:Survey data.  The REDS is the follow-up survey of the Additional Rural Incomes Survey (ARIS),
13:451:34798:16:17:72:540:321:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:which was first collected in 1971. The sample was designed to represent the rural population of India
13:452:34899:17:16:72:540:304:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:across 17 major states.  The ARIS covers 4,527 households in 259 villages.  Three follow-up rounds
13:453:34997:16:17:72:540:288:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:were collected in 1982, 1999, and 2006 to re-visit these households.  The sample was increased over
13:454:35096:17:16:72:540:271:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:time  by  randomly  sampling  additional  households  from  the  same  villages.   The  sample  in  2006
13:455:35200:16:16:72:540:255:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:consists of roughly 9,500 households in 242 villages.9We can only use the 2006 round because of
13:456:35295:16:17:72:259:239:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:changes in the questionnaire over time.
13:457:35334:17:16:88:539:222:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:There are three reasons for using the REDS data to study the questions outlined above.  First,
13:458:35428:16:17:72:540:206:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:the  geographic  coverage  of  almost  the  entire  country  allows  a  comparison  of  households  in  very
13:459:35536:17:16:72:540:189:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:different agro-climatic regions and economic conditions.  Second,  the REDS 2006 survey collects
13:460:35632:16:17:72:540:173:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:detailed information about time allocated to different activities for all household members.  This
13:461:35730:17:16:72:540:156:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:information is collected for three seasons of the year (each of which lasts for four months), which are
13:462:35833:16:15:84:539:140:6:g_d0_f6:9:g_d0_f7:7The estimation is restricted to children who are still in school in the follow-up round.  Summary statistics are
13:463:35946:15:7:72:231:125:9:g_d0_f7:9:g_d0_f7:reported in Table C.1 in the Appendix.
13:464:35984:7:15:84:539:118:6:g_d0_f6:9:g_d0_f7:8Attanasio et al. (2015) find a elasticity of substitution of 0.871 for the younger cohort and of 0.952 for the older
13:465:36101:15:11:72:540:103:9:g_d0_f7:9:g_d0_f7:cohort, and self-productivity parameters around 0.283 and 0.605 for the younger and the older cohorts, respectively.
13:466:36217:11:7:72:540:92:9:g_d0_f7:9:g_d0_f7:In Cunha et al. (2010), the estimated elasticity of substitution varies between 0.421 and 0.547 in the age group 5-14.
13:467:36335:7:0:84:539:85:6:g_d0_f6:9:g_d0_f7:9Due to armed conflict no data were collected in Jammu & Kashmir and in Assam in the 2006 round of interviews.
14:468:36447:0:17:72:540:709:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:also marked by very different levels of agricultural activity. Seasons 1 and 2 are the agricultural peak
14:469:36551:17:16:72:540:692:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:seasons in which most of the agricultural production takes place.  Season 3 is the dry season, during
14:470:36652:16:17:72:540:676:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:which only very few crops are cultivated and agricultural activity is considerably lower.  We restrict
14:471:36754:17:16:72:540:659:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:the sample to households and individuals with complete information on time  allocation,  income
14:472:36849:16:17:72:540:643:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:and consumption, and who live in rural areas.10In order to use the available information efficiently,
14:473:36950:17:16:72:540:626:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:we first generate a sample of mothers with children aged 6 to 16.  This sample consists of 4,356
14:474:37046:16:17:72:539:610:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:households and 4,651 mothers.11Table 2 reports some general household summary statistics for this
14:475:37143:17:16:72:540:593:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:sample.  We then restrict the sample to matched daughter-mother information, for all girls aged 6 to
14:476:37243:16:16:72:539:577:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:16, who still live with their mothers.12This final sample consists of 2,934 households, 3,040 mothers
14:477:37344:16:17:72:540:561:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:and 4,306 daughters.13The unit of observation in the main estimation is the daughter-mother match
14:478:37441:17:16:72:540:544:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:across three agricultural seasons, resulting in a final sample size of 12,918 observations.  Detailed
14:479:37542:16:32:72:491:528:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:summary statistics for mothers and daughters are reported in the Appendix, Table C.2.
14:480:37627:32:24:72:212:496:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:4.1    Risk in rural India
14:481:37653:24:16:72:540:472:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:In rural India, agriculture is the dominant source of income for the majority of households, either
14:482:37752:16:17:72:540:456:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:through own agricultural production or through casual agricultural employment.  Agriculture em-
14:483:37847:17:16:72:540:439:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:ploys 67% of all male workers and 83% of all female workers in 2004/05 (National Sample Survey
14:484:37941:16:17:72:540:423:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:Office, 2006).  In the REDS sample, 63% of households cultivate their own land, and labor income
14:485:38037:17:16:72:540:406:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:consists to 59% of income from casual employment in agriculture or own agricultural production
14:486:38131:16:17:72:540:390:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:(c.f. Table 2). In addition, employment in agriculture (own agricultural production and agricultural
14:487:38231:17:16:72:409:373:11:g_d0_f3:8:g_d0_f5:casual labor) makes up for 79% of the labor market time of mothers.14
14:488:38300:16:16:88:539:357:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:Agricultural income is inherently risky.  As previous literature has shown, wages and employ-
14:489:38393:16:17:72:540:341:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:ment levels in the agricultural sector are strongly influenced by rainfall conditions (see e.g. Jay-
14:490:38493:17:16:72:540:324:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:achandran, 2006; Kaur, 2014).  High rainfall leads to good harvests,  high demand for labor and
14:491:38588:16:17:72:540:308:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:high wages.  In contrast, low rainfall levels lead to poor harvests and low demand for agricultural
14:492:38687:17:16:72:540:291:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:labor.  The variability of rainfall combined with the village-level availability of irrigation systems
14:493:38789:16:17:72:405:275:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:should therefore be good proxies for consumption risk in this context.
14:494:38859:17:16:88:539:258:11:g_d0_f3:8:g_d0_f5:In order to predict consumption risk, we merge the REDS data with historical rainfall data.15
14:495:38952:16:16:72:540:242:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:We then compute consumption risk as follows.  First, we estimate in the cross-section how strongly
14:496:39050:16:17:72:540:226:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:current rainfall levels determine a household’s consumption per capita (in logs) given a village’s
14:497:39148:17:11:80:507:209:6:g_d0_f6:9:g_d0_f7:10We drop all households living in cities with more than 10,000 inhabitants, based on the Census in 2001.
14:498:39253:11:11:80:373:198:6:g_d0_f6:9:g_d0_f7:11We use this sample to calculate our risk variable and to predict wages.
14:499:39326:11:11:80:422:187:6:g_d0_f6:9:g_d0_f7:12We focus on the age group 6 to 16 because most girls still live at home at this age.
14:500:39412:11:15:80:539:176:6:g_d0_f6:9:g_d0_f7:13The final sample is considerably smaller than the original sample.  By far most households had to be dropped
14:501:39522:15:11:72:540:161:9:g_d0_f7:9:g_d0_f7:because they have no children in the relevant age group (≈2,500), or only boys (≈1,500).  Another 1,000 households
14:502:39636:11:11:71:539:150:9:g_d0_f7:9:g_d0_f7:had to be dropped because they live in cities with more than 10,000 inhabitants, and for roughly 1,000 households it
14:503:39752:11:7:71:351:139:9:g_d0_f7:9:g_d0_f7:was not possible to match mothers and daughters by their identifiers.
14:504:39821:7:11:80:260:132:6:g_d0_f6:9:g_d0_f7:14As reported in Table C.2 in the Appendix.
14:505:39864:11:14:80:539:121:6:g_d0_f6:9:g_d0_f7:15We use precipitation data compiled by the University of Delaware for the period 1960 to 2010.  Data are available
14:506:39979:14:11:71:539:107:9:g_d0_f7:9:g_d0_f7:for 1900 onwards, but the data quality improved a lot over the time period, which is why we prefer working only with
14:507:40095:11:11:71:539:96:9:g_d0_f7:9:g_d0_f7:more recent data.  We merge the data with the geocode of the village center.  Since the data are available for grids of
14:508:40214:11:0:71:483:85:9:g_d0_f7:9:g_d0_f7:0.5 degrees in latitude and longitude (approximately 50 km), some of the villages fall in the same cell.
15:509:40320:0:17:72:540:709:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:share of area that is irrigated.16Results are reported in Table 3.  Rainfall is interacted with the
15:510:40419:17:16:72:540:692:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:share of agricultural land under irrigation in the village, to capture differential risk exposure.  The
15:511:40522:16:17:72:540:676:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:assumption here is that rainfall shocks will translate less strongly into consumption outcomes the
15:512:40620:17:16:72:540:659:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:higher the share of irrigated land in a village is.  This can be because households are more likely to
15:513:40722:16:17:72:540:643:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:have irrigation on their own land in villages with a high share of area under irrigation or because
15:514:40821:17:16:72:540:626:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:casual agricultural employment will be less affected by current rainfall levels (since most farmers in
15:515:40923:16:17:72:540:610:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:the village do not depend on current rainfall levels for their agricultural output).  We also control
15:516:41024:17:16:72:540:593:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:for irrigation levels,  and average annual rainfall separately.  State fixed effects and a linear time
15:517:41126:16:16:72:540:577:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:trend are also included.  To gauge the robustness of these estimates, we add a number of controls
15:518:41223:16:17:72:540:561:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:that could proxy for permanent income such as education,  caste,  religion and land ownership in
15:519:41319:17:16:72:534:544:11:g_d0_f3:8:g_d0_f5:column (2).  As can be seen in Table 3, our results are robust to the inclusion of these controls.17
15:520:41419:16:17:88:539:528:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:Given these estimates, we then simulate the amount of risk faced by every household in a village
15:521:41515:17:16:72:540:511:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:using the historical rainfall distribution and the current share of area that is irrigated in each village.
15:522:41622:16:17:72:540:495:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:We use historical rainfall data to calculate the probability of each rainfall outcome.  We then predict
15:523:41725:17:16:72:540:478:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:a household’s log consumption per capita for each rainfall outcome given the current availability of
15:524:41825:16:16:72:540:462:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:irrigation in a village.  By multiplying the probability of each rainfall outcome with the predicted
15:525:41925:16:17:72:540:446:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:log consumption at that rainfall level, we obtain a probability distribution of consumption outcomes
15:526:42025:17:16:72:539:429:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:for each household in a particular village.18Finally,  we calculate the interquartile range as well
15:527:42124:16:17:72:540:413:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:as the standard deviation of a household’s predicted log consumption per capita as measures of
15:528:42218:17:32:72:157:396:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:consumption risk.
15:529:42235:32:23:72:355:364:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:4.2    Human capital accumulation in rural India
15:530:42283:23:17:72:540:341:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:Achieving universal education has been the declared goal of Indian governments since independence.
15:531:42381:17:16:72:540:324:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:The Right to Education Act of 2002 declares free and compulsory education a fundamental right
15:532:42474:16:17:72:540:308:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:of children aged 6 to 14.  Since then, substantial improvements have been made in the enrollment
15:533:42570:17:16:72:540:291:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:rates of boys and girls and in closing the gender gap in primary school enrollment.  By 2010/11
15:534:42665:16:17:72:540:275:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:the gross enrollment ratio reached 114.9% for boys and 116.3% for girls in the classes 1 to 5.  The
15:535:42764:17:16:72:540:258:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:gender gap has also been closing in middle school enrollment:  by 2010/11, 87.5% of the boys were
15:536:42861:16:16:72:495:242:11:g_d0_f3:8:g_d0_f5:enrolled in the classes 6 to 10, as compared to 82.9% of the girls (also in gross figures).19
15:537:42954:16:17:88:539:226:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:Still,  literacy rates remain low,  particularly for women and in rural areas.  According to the
15:538:43050:17:16:71:539:209:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:Census in 2011, only 50.6% of the women aged 15 and above living in rural India are literate.  This
15:539:43149:16:16:71:539:193:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:is  not  only  an  issue  among  the  adult  population:  according  to  the  Annual  Status  of  Education
15:540:43256:16:11:80:341:177:6:g_d0_f6:9:g_d0_f7:16This approach is inspired by Dercon and Christiaensen (2011).
15:541:43319:11:15:80:539:166:6:g_d0_f6:9:g_d0_f7:17Still we have to worry that these results are driven by differences in average consumption across villages that
15:542:43432:15:11:72:540:151:9:g_d0_f7:9:g_d0_f7:correlate  with  average  rainfall  levels  rather  than  current  shocks.   We  therefore  employ  two  additional  robustness
15:543:43559:11:11:72:540:140:9:g_d0_f7:9:g_d0_f7:checks.  First, we test if using the deviation in rainfall from the long-term average instead of current precipitation
15:544:43677:11:11:72:540:129:9:g_d0_f7:9:g_d0_f7:changes the results.  Second, we pool the 2006 data with the 2014 follow-up round and test if our results are sensitive
15:545:43796:11:11:72:540:118:9:g_d0_f7:9:g_d0_f7:to the inclusion of village fixed effects.  In both approaches,  the results are qualitatively similar to those reported
15:546:43916:11:7:72:292:107:9:g_d0_f7:9:g_d0_f7:here, and are available from the authors upon request.
15:547:43970:7:15:80:539:100:6:g_d0_f6:9:g_d0_f7:18The probability weights are obtained by dividing the sample rainfall distribution in 0.025 intervals of annual log
15:548:44086:15:7:72:506:85:9:g_d0_f7:9:g_d0_f7:rainfall.  We then calculate the historical probability of village-level rainfall to fall in each of these intervals.
15:549:44203:7:0:80:478:78:6:g_d0_f6:9:g_d0_f7:19Ministry of Human Resource Development, Government of India.  Data retrieved from Indiastat.
16:550:44299:0:17:72:540:709:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:Report (ASER) for Rural India, only 48.2% of the children in grade 5 could read a grade 2 level
16:551:44394:17:16:72:222:692:11:g_d0_f3:8:g_d0_f5:text in 2011 (Pratham, 2012).20
16:552:44425:16:17:88:539:676:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:While  the  quality  of  education  is  an  often-cited  reason  for  low  learning  outcomes  in  India
16:553:44529:17:16:72:540:659:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:(Kremer et al., 2005; Banerjee et al., 2010), demand-driven factors also play a role, particularly in
16:554:44630:16:17:72:540:643:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:the inequality of learning outcomes between boys and girls.  According to the Status of Education
16:555:44727:17:16:72:540:626:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:and Vocational Training in India survey conducted in 2011/12, the ratio of children not attending
16:556:44824:16:17:72:540:610:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:school in the age group 10 to 14 is highest for girls in rural areas (10.1%), as compared to 6.4%
16:557:44921:17:16:72:540:593:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:for girls in urban areas and 6.7% for boys in rural areas.  This inequality increases further if we
16:558:45020:16:16:72:540:577:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:consider the age group 15 to 19: in this group, 44.8% of the rural girls are out of school as compared
16:559:45122:16:17:72:540:561:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:to 29.7% of the urban girls and to 34.3% of the rural boys.  In the same survey, more than half
16:560:45217:17:16:72:540:544:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:of the girls aged 5 to 29 years who were currently not in school but ever attended an educational
16:561:45314:16:17:72:540:528:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:institution stated that attending domestic chores was the single reason for not being enrolled in
16:562:45411:17:16:72:390:511:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:any educational institution (National Sample Survey Office, 2015).
16:563:45477:16:17:88:539:495:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:In the REDS sample, 79% of the girls aged 6 to 16 are currently enrolled in school, as compared
16:564:45572:17:32:72:281:478:11:g_d0_f3:8:g_d0_f5:to 86% of the boys in the same age group.21
16:565:45615:32:23:72:433:446:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:4.3    Time allocation of mothers and daughters in rural India
16:566:45677:23:17:72:540:423:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:The REDS collects two different types of time allocation data.  The demographic section records the
16:567:45776:17:16:72:540:406:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:number of hours per day allocated to different activities on a typical day for all household members
16:568:45876:16:17:72:540:390:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:in each of three agricultural seasons.  This section explicitly differentiates between time in school,
16:569:45978:17:16:72:540:373:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:time allocated to household chores, market work and leisure.  Consistent with the ILO definition, we
16:570:46078:16:16:72:540:357:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:define labor supply as the total number of hours per season worked in paid employment or in self-
16:571:46175:16:17:72:540:341:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:employment.  However, we exclude hours worked in own-agricultural production from this variable.
16:572:46271:17:16:72:540:324:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:The reason for this is that labor supply to own-agricultural production will be affected by rainfall
16:573:46371:16:17:72:539:308:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:risk in different ways and we want to avoid mixing up different causal mechanisms.22Household
16:574:46464:17:16:72:540:291:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:chores, then, include all activities related to the household:  construction and maintenance of the
16:575:46563:16:17:72:540:275:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:house, farm and other assets, household work and other household-related activities (collecting fuel,
16:576:46664:17:16:72:540:258:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:herding cattle,  fishing,  cutting grass).  We also count the time allocated to livestock production
16:577:46764:16:16:72:540:242:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:as home production, arguing that livestock production is mostly a household duty, even though it
16:578:46860:16:17:72:241:226:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:could also be undertaken for profit.
16:579:46896:17:16:88:539:209:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:As reported in Table 4,  girls in our sample spend on average 6.2 hours per day in school or
16:580:46988:16:17:72:540:193:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:studying in season 1 (one of the two agricultural peak seasons).  Girls also spend 1.6 hours per day
16:581:47088:17:16:72:540:176:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:on household chores, but only 0.1 hours per day working on the labor market.  This exemplifies why
16:582:47186:16:14:80:539:160:6:g_d0_f6:9:g_d0_f7:20ASER is based on an annual survey that assesses children’s schooling status and basic learning levels throughout
16:583:47300:14:11:72:540:146:9:g_d0_f7:9:g_d0_f7:all rural districts of India.  It is facilitated by the Indian NGO Pratham and interviews are conducted by volunteers,
16:584:47418:11:11:72:540:135:9:g_d0_f7:9:g_d0_f7:which has raised a number of doubts regarding data quality.  Still, it is the only India-wide assessment of learning
16:585:47534:11:8:72:173:124:9:g_d0_f7:9:g_d0_f7:levels currently available.
16:586:47561:8:10:80:467:116:6:g_d0_f6:9:g_d0_f7:21Summary statistics are reported in the Appendix:  Table C.2 for girls, and Table C.3 for boys.
16:587:47657:10:15:80:539:106:6:g_d0_f6:9:g_d0_f7:22Labor  supply  to  own-agricultural  production  should  fall  with  increasing  rainfall  risk,  as  this  income  source
16:588:47781:15:11:72:540:91:9:g_d0_f7:9:g_d0_f7:becomes increasingly risky.  Labor has to be allocated to agricultural production partly before the rainfall realizes,
16:589:47899:11:0:72:489:80:9:g_d0_f7:9:g_d0_f7:hence before the household can assess how the harvest, and therefore the returns to that labor, will be.
17:590:48005:0:17:72:540:709:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:we need to take into account intra-household substitution effects when thinking about the effect of
17:591:48104:17:16:72:251:692:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:shocks on the time allocation of girls.
17:592:48143:16:17:88:539:676:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:For all household members aged 10 to 65, the questionnaire also collects information about the
17:593:48237:17:16:72:539:659:11:g_d0_f3:8:g_d0_f5:actual number of days and hours per day allocated to a number of different activities per season.23
17:594:48336:16:17:72:540:643:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:We use this section to compute time allocation variables for mothers.  The labor force participation
17:595:48436:17:16:72:540:626:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:in our sample of mothers is 21% in the agricultural peak seasons and 15% in the lean season (c.f.
17:596:48533:16:17:72:540:610:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:Table 4).24In all seasons, labor supply is on average slightly higher than time allocated to own-
17:597:48630:17:16:72:540:593:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:agricultural production.  By far the majority of maternal time is spent on household chores:  701
17:598:48727:16:16:72:540:577:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:hours  in  season  1,  which  corresponds  to  roughly  43.8  hours  per  week.   Hours  allocated  to  labor
17:599:48836:16:17:72:540:561:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:supply and own-agricultural production together make up for about 9.4 hours per week on average
17:600:48931:17:16:72:540:544:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:in season 1.  Total time spent on activities other than leisure is thus 53.2 hours per week in season
17:601:49032:16:17:72:540:528:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:1,  which  is  very  close  to  the  time  spent  on  those  activities  reported  in  the  Time  Use  Survey  of
17:602:49145:17:16:72:152:511:11:g_d0_f3:8:g_d0_f5:1998/99 (53.4).25
17:603:49162:16:17:88:539:495:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:Because this section does not collect time in school or studying, we cannot use this information
17:604:49258:17:16:72:540:478:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:to  test  for  the  effects  of  risk  on  time  in  school.   However  having  information  on  time  allocated
17:605:49370:16:16:72:540:462:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:to  household  chores  from  two  different  sections,  allows  to  test  if  our  results  are  robust  to  using
17:606:49484:16:17:72:540:446:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:alternative  time  measures.   With  respect  to  time  spent  on  household  chores  by  girls,  we  find
17:607:49590:17:16:72:540:429:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:correlation coefficients of 0.83, 0.81 and 0.81 between the two time measures for seasons 1, 2 and
17:608:49688:16:38:72:150:413:11:g_d0_f3:8:g_d0_f5:3, respectively.26
17:609:49706:38:28:72:236:375:14:g_d0_f2:14:g_d0_f2:5    Estimation strategy
17:610:49730:28:29:72:306:347:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:The structural hours function to be estimated is:
17:611:49779:29:30:99:539:318:11:g_d0_f8:11:g_d0_f3:hijt=β0+β1Rjt+β2lnwijt+β3Nijt+β4Xijt+-ijt,(20)
17:612:49825:30:17:72:540:288:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:where the dependent variable is the amount of time childi, living in villagej, allocates to schooling
17:613:49926:17:16:72:540:271:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:(or to home production) at timet.  We are particularly interested in estimatingβ1, i.e.  the effect
17:614:50025:16:16:72:539:255:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:of  consumption  riskRjton  hours  allocated  to  different  activities.   As  discussed  previously,  this
17:615:50132:16:17:72:540:239:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:variable is predicted at the village level.  The identifying assumption is that,  conditional on the
17:616:50232:17:16:72:540:222:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:control variables, no other (unobserved) characteristics that determine hours in school are correlated
17:617:50334:16:16:72:540:206:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:with our regressor of interest, i.e.  consumption risk.  This assumption is quite strong and we will
17:618:50434:16:15:80:539:190:6:g_d0_f6:9:g_d0_f7:23The full list of activities are salaried work,  agricultural casual labor,  own-crop production,  own-livestock pro-
17:619:50552:15:11:72:540:175:9:g_d0_f7:9:g_d0_f7:duction, work for public works programs, non-agricultural casual labor, migration, self-employment in non-farming,
17:620:50666:11:11:72:540:164:9:g_d0_f7:9:g_d0_f7:construction and maintenance of house,  farm and other assets,  household work,  other household-related activities
17:621:50781:11:7:72:512:153:9:g_d0_f7:9:g_d0_f7:(collecting fuel, herding cattle, fishing, cutting grass) and other activities (schooling, unemployment, leisure).
17:622:50895:7:15:80:539:146:6:g_d0_f6:9:g_d0_f7:24This is slightly lower than the 24.5% reported for rural India by the NSSO in 2009/10.  But the NSSO classifies
17:623:51008:15:7:72:320:131:9:g_d0_f7:9:g_d0_f7:own-agricultural production as labor supply, while we do not.
17:624:51069:7:15:80:539:124:6:g_d0_f6:9:g_d0_f7:25There is a substantial time lag between the two surveys,  however.  Also our sample consists only of mothers,
17:625:51180:15:7:72:254:109:9:g_d0_f7:9:g_d0_f7:while the TUS surveys women aged 15 to 65.
